<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Water-fluoridation-extent-world-equirectangular.svg"><img alt="Water-fluoridation-extent-world-equirectangular.svg" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="433" data-file-width="856" decoding="async" height="111" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1c/Water-fluoridation-extent-world-equirectangular.svg/220px-Water-fluoridation-extent-world-equirectangular.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1c/Water-fluoridation-extent-world-equirectangular.svg/330px-Water-fluoridation-extent-world-equirectangular.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1c/Water-fluoridation-extent-world-equirectangular.svg/440px-Water-fluoridation-extent-world-equirectangular.svg.png 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Water-fluoridation-extent-world-equirectangular.svg" title="Enlarge"></a></div></div></div></div>
<p><a href="/wiki/Water_fluoridation" title="Water fluoridation">Water fluoridation</a> is the controlled addition of <a href="/wiki/Fluoride" title="Fluoride">fluoride</a> to a <a href="/wiki/Water_supply" title="Water supply">public water supply</a> to reduce <a href="/wiki/Tooth_decay" title="Tooth decay">tooth decay</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FRWG_1-0"><a href="#cite_note-FRWG-1">[1]</a></sup> and is handled differently by country. Fluoridated water has fluoride at a level that is proven effective for preventing cavities; this can occur naturally or by adding fluoride.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FRWG_1-1"><a href="#cite_note-FRWG-1">[1]</a></sup> Fluoridated water operates on tooth surfaces: in the mouth it creates low levels of fluoride in <a href="/wiki/Saliva" title="Saliva">saliva</a>, which reduces the rate at which <a href="/wiki/Tooth_enamel" title="Tooth enamel">tooth enamel</a> demineralizes and increases the rate at which it <a href="/wiki/Remineralisation_of_teeth" title="Remineralisation of teeth">remineralizes</a> in the early stages of cavities.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Pizzo_2-0"><a href="#cite_note-Pizzo-2">[2]</a></sup> Typically a fluoridated compound is added to <a href="/wiki/Drinking_water" title="Drinking water">drinking water</a>, a process that in the U.S. costs an average of about $1.17 per person-year.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FRWG_1-2"><a href="#cite_note-FRWG-1">[1]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-inflation-US_3-0"><a href="#cite_note-inflation-US-3">[3]</a></sup> Defluoridation is needed when the naturally occurring fluoride level exceeds recommended limits.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Taricska_4-0"><a href="#cite_note-Taricska-4">[4]</a></sup> In 2011 the <a href="/wiki/World_Health_Organization" title="World Health Organization">World Health Organization</a> suggested a level of fluoride from 0.5 to 1.5 mg/L (milligrams per litre), depending on climate, local environment, and other sources of fluoride.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-WHO2011_5-0"><a href="#cite_note-WHO2011-5">[5]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Bottled_water" title="Bottled water">Bottled water</a> typically has unknown fluoride levels.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Hobson_6-0"><a href="#cite_note-Hobson-6">[6]</a></sup>
</p><p><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Dental_cavities" title="Dental cavities">Dental cavities</a> remains a major <a href="/wiki/Public_health" title="Public health">public health</a> concern in most <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Industrialized_countries" title="Industrialized countries">industrialized countries</a>, affecting 60–90% of schoolchildren and the vast majority of adults.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Petersen-2004_7-0"><a href="#cite_note-Petersen-2004-7">[7]</a></sup> Water fluoridation reduces cavities in children, while efficacy in adults is less clear.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Cochrane2015_8-0"><a href="#cite_note-Cochrane2015-8">[8]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Scher2011_9-0"><a href="#cite_note-Scher2011-9">[9]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Tienman2013_10-0"><a href="#cite_note-Tienman2013-10">[10]</a></sup> A Cochrane review estimates a reduction in cavities when water fluoridation was used by children who had no access to other sources of fluoride to be 35% in baby teeth and 26% in permanent teeth.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Cochrane2015_8-1"><a href="#cite_note-Cochrane2015-8">[8]</a></sup> Most European countries have experienced substantial declines in tooth decay without its use. Recent studies suggest that water fluoridation, particularly in industrialized countries, may be unnecessary because topical fluorides (such as in toothpaste) are widely used and cavity rates have become low.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Pizzo_2-1"><a href="#cite_note-Pizzo-2">[2]</a></sup> For this reason, some scientists consider fluoridation to be unethical due to "the debilitating effects of <a href="/wiki/Fluoride_toxicity" title="Fluoride toxicity">fluoride toxicity</a>" and the lack of <a href="/wiki/Informed_consent" title="Informed consent">informed consent</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Unde-2018_11-0"><a href="#cite_note-Unde-2018-11">[11]</a></sup>
</p><p>Although fluoridation can cause <a href="/wiki/Dental_fluorosis" title="Dental fluorosis">dental fluorosis</a>, which can alter the appearance of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Tooth_development" title="Tooth development">developing teeth</a> or <a href="/wiki/Tooth_enamel" title="Tooth enamel">enamel fluorosis</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Pizzo_2-2"><a href="#cite_note-Pizzo-2">[2]</a></sup> the differences are mild and usually not considered to be of aesthetic or public-health concern.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NHMRC_12-0"><a href="#cite_note-NHMRC-12">[12]</a></sup> There is no clear evidence of other <a href="/wiki/Adverse_effect" title="Adverse effect">adverse effects</a> from water fluoridation, as revealed by the York review from 2000.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-YorkReview2000_13-0"><a href="#cite_note-YorkReview2000-13">[13]</a></sup> A 2007 Australian systematic review used the same inclusion criteria as York's, plus one additional study. This did not affect the York conclusions.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-14"><a href="#cite_note-14">[14]</a></sup> Fluoride's effects depend on the total daily intake of fluoride from all sources. Drinking water is typically the largest source;<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Fawell_15-0"><a href="#cite_note-Fawell-15">[15]</a></sup> other methods of <a href="/wiki/Fluoride_therapy" title="Fluoride therapy">fluoride therapy</a> include fluoridation of toothpaste, salt, and milk.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Jones-PH_16-0"><a href="#cite_note-Jones-PH-16">[16]</a></sup> The views on the most effective method for community prevention of tooth decay are mixed. The Australian government states that water fluoridation is the most effective means of achieving fluoride exposure that is community-wide.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NHMRC_12-1"><a href="#cite_note-NHMRC-12">[12]</a></sup> The World Health Organization states water fluoridation, when feasible and culturally acceptable, has substantial advantages, especially for subgroups at high risk,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Petersen-2004_7-1"><a href="#cite_note-Petersen-2004-7">[7]</a></sup> while the <a href="/wiki/European_Commission" title="European Commission">European Commission</a> finds no advantage to water fluoridation compared with topical use.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-EU2011_17-0"><a href="#cite_note-EU2011-17">[17]</a></sup>
</p><p>Currently about 372 million people (around 5.7% of the world population) receive artificially-fluoridated water in about 24 countries, including Australia, Brazil, Canada, Chile, Republic of Ireland, Malaysia, the U.S., and Vietnam.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-extent_18-0"><a href="#cite_note-extent-18">[18]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-EndFinIsrael0_19-0"><a href="#cite_note-EndFinIsrael0-19">[19]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Cheng2007_20-0"><a href="#cite_note-Cheng2007-20">[20]</a></sup> 57.4 million people receive naturally occurring fluoridated water at or above optimal levels in countries such as Sweden, China, Sri Lanka, Finland, Zimbabwe and Gabon.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-extent_18-1"><a href="#cite_note-extent-18">[18]</a></sup> Community water fluoridation is rare in <a href="/wiki/Continental_Europe" title="Continental Europe">Continental Europe</a>, with 97–98% choosing not to fluoridate drinking water.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-21"><a href="#cite_note-21">[21]</a></sup> Fluoridated salt and milk is promoted in some European countries instead. Water fluoridation has been replaced by other modes in many countries where water supplies are too decentralized for it to be a practical choice, or existing natural fluoride levels were already ample, including Germany, Finland, Japan, Netherlands, Sweden, Switzerland,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Cheng2007_20-1"><a href="#cite_note-Cheng2007-20">[20]</a></sup> Denmark and at a time Israel.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-EndFinIsrael0_19-1"><a href="#cite_note-EndFinIsrael0-19">[19]</a></sup> Cessation of water fluoridation has been demonstrated in scientific studies such as a recent one in Calgary, Alberta, to result in increased rates of dental decay.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-22"><a href="#cite_note-22">[22]</a></sup> While fluoridation can result in mild dental fluorosis, this effect is barely detectable and causes no concerns with the appearance or health of teeth. Countries practicing artificial water fluoridation vary in their recommended fluoride levels according to what health authorities in each have determined to be most effective for its citizens. The US recently reset the recommended optimal level of fluoride in drinking water, lowering it slightly, because of observed increased <a href="/wiki/Dental_fluorosis" title="Dental fluorosis">Fluorosis</a> levels, likely due to additional fluoride sources like toothpaste and mouthwash which were not present when this level was originally set.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-23"><a href="#cite_note-23">[23]</a></sup>
</p>
<style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r1044870489">@media all and (max-width:720px){body.skin-minerva .mw-parser-output .tocright{display:none}.mw-parser-output .tocright{width:100%!important}}@media all and (min-width:720px){.mw-parser-output .tocright{float:right;clear:right;width:auto;margin:0 0 0.5em 1em}.mw-parser-output .tocright-clear-left{clear:left}.mw-parser-output .tocright-clear-both{clear:both}.mw-parser-output .tocright-clear-none{clear:none}}</style><div class="tocright"><meta property="mw:PageProp/toc"/></div>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Africa">Africa</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Water_fluoridation_by_country&amp;action=edit&amp;section=1" title="Edit section: Africa">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>Of Africa's 1.1 billion people, about 400,000 get artificially-fluoridated water (in Libya, data pre-2003).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-OIMextent0_24-0"><a href="#cite_note-OIMextent0-24">[24]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Egypt">Egypt</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Water_fluoridation_by_country&amp;action=edit&amp;section=2" title="Edit section: Egypt">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Water is fluoridated in <a href="/wiki/Egypt" title="Egypt">Egypt</a>, with no recommended minimum value but with a maximum value of 0.4 mg/L.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Libya">Libya</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Water_fluoridation_by_country&amp;action=edit&amp;section=3" title="Edit section: Libya">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Before 2003, 400,000 Libyans were receiving artificially-fluoridated water.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-OIMextent0_24-1"><a href="#cite_note-OIMextent0-24">[24]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Nigeria">Nigeria</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Water_fluoridation_by_country&amp;action=edit&amp;section=4" title="Edit section: Nigeria">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Only a fraction of Nigerians receive water from waterworks, so water fluoridation affects very few people. A 2009 study found that about 21% of water sources naturally contain fluoride to the recommended range of 0.3–0.6 ppm. About 62% have fluoride below this range.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-25"><a href="#cite_note-25">[25]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="South_Africa">South Africa</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Water_fluoridation_by_country&amp;action=edit&amp;section=5" title="Edit section: South Africa">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p><a href="/wiki/South_Africa" title="South Africa">South Africa</a>'s Health Department recommends adding fluoridation chemicals to drinking water in some areas. It also advises removal of fluoride from drinking water (defluoridation) where the fluoride content is too high.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-26"><a href="#cite_note-26">[26]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-27"><a href="#cite_note-27">[27]</a></sup>
</p><p>Legislation around mandatory fluoridation was introduced in 2002, but has been delayed since then pending further research after opposition from water companies, municipalities and the public.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-28"><a href="#cite_note-28">[28]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Zambia">Zambia</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Water_fluoridation_by_country&amp;action=edit&amp;section=6" title="Edit section: Zambia">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Approximately 947,000 (7% of the population) receives water with naturally occurring fluoride in it.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-extent_18-2"><a href="#cite_note-extent-18">[18]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Zimbabwe">Zimbabwe</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Water_fluoridation_by_country&amp;action=edit&amp;section=7" title="Edit section: Zimbabwe">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Roughly 2,600,000 (21% of the population) receives water with naturally occurring fluoride in it.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-extent_18-3"><a href="#cite_note-extent-18">[18]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Asia">Asia</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Water_fluoridation_by_country&amp;action=edit&amp;section=8" title="Edit section: Asia">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="China">China</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Water_fluoridation_by_country&amp;action=edit&amp;section=9" title="Edit section: China">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Many areas in China have fluoride at levels far higher than recommended due to natural occurrence or industrial contamination, which has resulted in high incidences<sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="margin-left:0.1em; white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Manual_of_Style" title="Wikipedia:Manual of Style"><span title="''incidence'' is normally used only in the singular form, perhaps ''incidence'', ''incidents'', or ''instances'' was intended (September 2022)">spelling?</span></a></i>]</sup> of skeletal fluorosis.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-scmp.com_29-0"><a href="#cite_note-scmp.com-29">[29]</a></sup> Water fluoridation levels are set at a national standard of 1 mg/L, with higher levels for rural areas at 1.2 mg/L.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-30"><a href="#cite_note-30">[30]</a></sup> Water fluoridation began in 1965 in the urban area of <a href="/wiki/Guangzhou" title="Guangzhou">Guangzhou</a>. It was interrupted during 1976–1978 due to the shortage of sodium silico-fluoride. It was resumed only in the <a href="/wiki/Fangcun,_Guangzhou" title="Fangcun, Guangzhou">Fangcun</a> district of the city, but was halted in 1983 after opponents claimed that fluoride levels were already sufficiently high in local foods and tea. Later analysis in 1988 found that the incidence of dental caries among 4-year-old children had increased by 62%. The fluoridation reduced the number of cavities, but increased <a href="/wiki/Dental_fluorosis" title="Dental fluorosis">dental fluorosis</a>; the fluoride levels could have been set too high, and low-quality equipment led to inconsistent, and often excessive, fluoride concentrations.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-31"><a href="#cite_note-31">[31]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Hong_Kong">Hong Kong</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Water_fluoridation_by_country&amp;action=edit&amp;section=10" title="Edit section: Hong Kong">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>All <a href="/wiki/Hong_Kong" title="Hong Kong">Hong Kong</a> residents receive natural occurring fluoride in water,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-OIMextent0_24-2"><a href="#cite_note-OIMextent0-24">[24]</a></sup> at about half the traditionally-recommended fluoride level. The <a href="/wiki/Water_Supplies_Department" title="Water Supplies Department">Water Supplies Department</a> fluoridates rainwater from 17 local reservoirs, in 21 treatment plants.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-32"><a href="#cite_note-32">[32]</a></sup> Recent tests showed drinking water to have an average fluoride level of 0.48 mg/L, and a maximum of 0.69 mg/L.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-33"><a href="#cite_note-33">[33]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="India">India</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Water_fluoridation_by_country&amp;action=edit&amp;section=11" title="Edit section: India">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Water fluoridation is not practiced in India.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-34"><a href="#cite_note-34">[34]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-35"><a href="#cite_note-35">[35]</a></sup> Due to naturally-occurring fluoride, both <a href="/wiki/Skeletal_fluorosis" title="Skeletal fluorosis">skeletal</a> and <a href="/wiki/Dental_fluorosis" title="Dental fluorosis">dental fluorosis</a> have been <a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Fluorosis_in_India&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Fluorosis in India (page does not exist)">endemic in India</a> in at least 20 states, including <a href="/wiki/Uttarakhand" title="Uttarakhand">Uttarakhand</a>, <a href="/wiki/Jharkhand" title="Jharkhand">Jharkhand</a> and <a href="/wiki/Chhattisgarh" title="Chhattisgarh">Chhattisgarh</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-36"><a href="#cite_note-36">[36]</a></sup> The maximum permissible limit of fluoride in drinking water in India is 1.2 mg/L,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-autogenerated9_37-0"><a href="#cite_note-autogenerated9-37">[37]</a></sup> and the government has been obligated to install fluoride removal plants of various technologies to reduce fluoride levels from industrial waste and mineral deposits. Now reverse osmosis plants are widely used. Household and public system reverse osmosis plants are common in the market. Alleppey in Kerala is most affected with over-fluoridated water. Government-installed reverse osmosis plants supply free filtered water. Rotary International Club, Saratoga USA, helped to install 3 RO Plants in rural Alleppey.
</p><p>As of 2014<sup class="plainlinks noexcerpt noprint asof-tag update" style="display:none;"><a class="external text" href="https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Water_fluoridation_by_country&amp;action=edit">[update]</a></sup>, there are 14,132 habitations in 19 States still containing fluoride above the permissible levels in drinking water. <a href="/wiki/Rajasthan" title="Rajasthan">Rajasthan</a> has the highest number of habitations (7,670) with high amount of fluoride in drinking water. <a href="/wiki/Telangana" title="Telangana">Telangana</a> has 1174, <a href="/wiki/Karnataka" title="Karnataka">Karnataka</a> has 1122 and Madhya Pradesh has 1055 habitation. Assam, Andhra Pradesh, Bihar, Chhattisgarh, Maharashtra, Odisha, West Bengal and Uttar Pradesh also has such habitations.
</p><p>The government of India launched the National Programme for Prevention and Control of Fluorosis in 2008–2009. In 2013–2014, the programme was brought under the National Rural Health Mission, which has so far covered 111 districts. The programme includes surveillance of fluorosis in the community, training and manpower support, establishment of diagnostic facilities, treatment and health education. The Indian Council of Medical Research has formed a task force on fluorosis to address issues related to prevention and control.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-The_Hindu14_38-0"><a href="#cite_note-The_Hindu14-38">[38]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Israel">Israel</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Water_fluoridation_by_country&amp;action=edit&amp;section=12" title="Edit section: Israel">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Fluoride was required in water supplies nationwide by legislation passed in 2002,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Zusman0_39-0"><a href="#cite_note-Zusman0-39">[39]</a></sup> but the requirement was repealed in 2014, and artificial fluoridation was disparaged by national health officials, effectively ending the practice in Israel for a short while.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-EndFinIsrael0_19-2"><a href="#cite_note-EndFinIsrael0-19">[19]</a></sup> After the election of 2015 the fluoridation program is to be re-debated the new deputy Health Minister <a href="/wiki/Yaakov_Litzman" title="Yaakov Litzman">Yaakov Litzman</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Litzman00_40-0"><a href="#cite_note-Litzman00-40">[40]</a></sup>
</p><p>Mekorot, Israel's national water company states, "In the South of the country, it is unnecessary to add fluoride because it is found naturally in the water."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Mekerot00_41-0"><a href="#cite_note-Mekerot00-41">[41]</a></sup> Water fluoridation was introduced in Israel's large cities in 1981, and a national effort to fluoridate all the country's water was approved in 1988.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-INNlev00_42-0"><a href="#cite_note-INNlev00-42">[42]</a></sup>
</p><p>In 2002, the Union of Local Authorities (ULA) and others petitioned Israel's High Court to stop the Health Ministry from forcing cities to implement water fluoridation. The court soon issued a restraining order,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-INN01_43-0"><a href="#cite_note-INN01-43">[43]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-INN02_44-0"><a href="#cite_note-INN02-44">[44]</a></sup> but after half a year ULA withdrew its petition upon the request of the court.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Haaretz00_45-0"><a href="#cite_note-Haaretz00-45">[45]</a></sup>
</p><p>By 2011, about 65% of the municipalities and local authorities in Israel had agreed to allow fluoridation, and there was active opposition to the spread of fluoridation to the towns where it has not yet been instituted.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-INNlev00_42-1"><a href="#cite_note-INNlev00-42">[42]</a></sup> In 2011, the Health and Welfare Committee of the <a href="/wiki/Knesset" title="Knesset">Knesset</a> criticized the Health Ministry for continuation of water fluoridation.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-INN03_46-0"><a href="#cite_note-INN03-46">[46]</a></sup>
</p><p>On 26 August 2014, Israel officially stopped adding fluoride to its water supplies.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-47"><a href="#cite_note-47">[47]</a></sup> According to a <a href="/wiki/Ministry_of_Health_(Israel)" title="Ministry of Health (Israel)">Ministry of Health</a> press release statement, the reasons it ended water fluoridation were: "Only some 1% of the water is used for drinking, while 99% of the water is intended for other uses (industry, agriculture, flushing toilets etc.). There is also scientific evidence that fluoride in large amounts can lead to damage to health. When fluoride is supplied via drinking water, there is no control regarding the amount of fluoride actually consumed, which could lead to excessive consumption. Supply of fluoridated water forces those who do not so wish to also consume water with added fluoride."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-EndFinIsrael0_19-3"><a href="#cite_note-EndFinIsrael0-19">[19]</a></sup> Many in the medical and dental communities in Israel criticized the decision as a mistake.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-48"><a href="#cite_note-48">[48]</a></sup>
</p><p>After the election of 2015, the new deputy Health Minister <a href="/wiki/Yaakov_Litzman" title="Yaakov Litzman">Yaakov Litzman</a> announced that the fluoridation program will be re-debated.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Litzman00_40-1"><a href="#cite_note-Litzman00-40">[40]</a></sup>
</p><p>As of July 2021, although in practice there is no legal impediment today to the return of drinking water fluoridation, it has not yet been returned in practice, and for about seven years there has been no drinking water fluoridation in Israel.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-49"><a href="#cite_note-49">[49]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Japan">Japan</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Water_fluoridation_by_country&amp;action=edit&amp;section=13" title="Edit section: Japan">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>The first community water fluoridation programme was in Kyoto prefecture in 1952, lasting 13 years. The second was established by US military authorities in Okinawa prefecture in 1957, lasting 15 years. The last experience was in Mie Prefecture in 1967, lasting 4 years.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-50"><a href="#cite_note-50">[50]</a></sup>
</p><p>Less than 1% of Japan practices water fluoridation.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NCFPR_51-0"><a href="#cite_note-NCFPR-51">[51]</a></sup> Instead, as of March 2010, a total of 7,479 schools and 777,596 preschool to junior high school children were participating in school-based fluoride mouth-rinsing programme (S-FMR), with an estimate of 2,000,000 children participating in 2020.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-52"><a href="#cite_note-52">[52]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="South_Korea">South Korea</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Water_fluoridation_by_country&amp;action=edit&amp;section=14" title="Edit section: South Korea">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>In 2005, the ruling <a href="/wiki/Uri_Party" title="Uri Party">Uri Party</a> proposed legislation for compulsory water fluoridation for municipalities. The legislation failed, and only 29 out of around 250 municipal governments had introduced water fluoridation at that time.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-KT2005_53-0"><a href="#cite_note-KT2005-53">[53]</a></sup> Fluoridation was proposed again in 2012.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-KoreaOoska_54-0"><a href="#cite_note-KoreaOoska-54">[54]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Malaysia">Malaysia</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Water_fluoridation_by_country&amp;action=edit&amp;section=15" title="Edit section: Malaysia">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>In 1998, 66% of Malaysians were getting fluoridated water.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NST00_55-0"><a href="#cite_note-NST00-55">[55]</a></sup>
</p><p>In 2010, Bernama reported, "Principal Director (Oral Health) in the Health Ministry, Datuk Dr Norain Abu Taib said that only 75.5% of the country's population are enjoying the benefits of water fluoridation".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-StarMy00_56-0"><a href="#cite_note-StarMy00-56">[56]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Singapore">Singapore</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Water_fluoridation_by_country&amp;action=edit&amp;section=16" title="Edit section: Singapore">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>In 1956, <a href="/wiki/Singapore" title="Singapore">Singapore</a> was the first Asian country to institute a water fluoridation program that covered 100% of the population.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-57"><a href="#cite_note-57">[57]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-58"><a href="#cite_note-58">[58]</a></sup> Water is fluoridated to a typical value of 0.4-0.6 mg per litre.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-59"><a href="#cite_note-59">[59]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Vietnam">Vietnam</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Water_fluoridation_by_country&amp;action=edit&amp;section=17" title="Edit section: Vietnam">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Only about 4% of the population of <a href="/wiki/Vietnam" title="Vietnam">Vietnam</a> has water fluoridation, whereas only 70% get their water from public supplies.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-OIMextent0_24-3"><a href="#cite_note-OIMextent0-24">[24]</a></sup> Many places in Vietnam there were already sufficient levels of fluoride or in some cases, fluoride concentrations were already too high and needed to be reduced to avoid the effects of fluorosis.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-60"><a href="#cite_note-60">[60]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Europe">Europe</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Water_fluoridation_by_country&amp;action=edit&amp;section=18" title="Edit section: Europe">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>Out of a population of about three-quarters of a billion, under 14 million people (approximately 2%) in Europe receive artificially-fluoridated water. Those people are in the UK (5,797,000), Republic of Ireland (4,780,000), Spain (4,250,000), and Serbia (300,000).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-OIMextent0_24-4"><a href="#cite_note-OIMextent0-24">[24]</a></sup>
</p><p>The first water fluoridation in Europe was in <a href="/wiki/West_Germany" title="West Germany">West Germany</a> and Sweden in 1952, bringing fluoridated water to about 42,000 people. By mid-1962, about 1 million Europeans in 18 communities in 11 countries were receiving fluoridated water.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-EuropeEarlyFluoride_61-0"><a href="#cite_note-EuropeEarlyFluoride-61">[61]</a></sup>
</p><p>Many European countries have rejected water fluoridation, including: Austria, Belgium, Finland, France, Germany, Hungary, Luxembourg, Netherlands, Northern Ireland, Norway, Sweden, Switzerland,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-SLW00_62-0"><a href="#cite_note-SLW00-62">[62]</a></sup> Scotland,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-BBCscot00_63-0"><a href="#cite_note-BBCscot00-63">[63]</a></sup> Iceland, and Italy.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-64"><a href="#cite_note-64">[64]</a></sup> A 2003 survey of over 500 Europeans from 16 countries concluded that "the vast majority of people opposed water fluoridation".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-PMgrif00_65-0"><a href="#cite_note-PMgrif00-65">[65]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Austria">Austria</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Water_fluoridation_by_country&amp;action=edit&amp;section=19" title="Edit section: Austria">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Austria has never implemented fluoridation due to an adequate level of fluoride in drinking water according to a study conducted in 1993. (Nell A, Sperr W. Fluoridgehaltuntersuchung des Trinkwassers in Osterreich 1993 [Analysis of the fluoride content of drinking water in Austria 1993]. Wien Klin Wochenschr. 1994;106(19):608-14. German. PMID 7998407.)
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Belgium">Belgium</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Water_fluoridation_by_country&amp;action=edit&amp;section=20" title="Edit section: Belgium">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Belgium does not fluoridate its water supply, although legislation permits it.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NCFPR_51-1"><a href="#cite_note-NCFPR-51">[51]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Czech_Republic">Czech Republic</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Water_fluoridation_by_country&amp;action=edit&amp;section=21" title="Edit section: Czech Republic">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Czech Republic (previously <a href="/wiki/Czechoslovakia" title="Czechoslovakia">Czechoslovakia</a>) started water fluoridation in 1958 in <a href="/wiki/T%C3%A1bor" title="Tábor">Tábor</a>. In <a href="/wiki/Prague" title="Prague">Prague</a> fluoridation started in 1975. It was stopped in Prague in 1988 and subsequently in the whole country. Since 2008 no water has been fluoridated.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-66"><a href="#cite_note-66">[66]</a></sup> Fluoridated salt is available.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-67"><a href="#cite_note-67">[67]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Croatia">Croatia</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Water_fluoridation_by_country&amp;action=edit&amp;section=22" title="Edit section: Croatia">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Croatia does not fluoridate its water.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-68"><a href="#cite_note-68">[68]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Denmark">Denmark</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Water_fluoridation_by_country&amp;action=edit&amp;section=23" title="Edit section: Denmark">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Denmark has released test results for levels of various water contaminants, including fluoride, in the drinking water of some cities: Copenhagen,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-69"><a href="#cite_note-69">[69]</a></sup> Brøndby,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-70"><a href="#cite_note-70">[70]</a></sup> Albertslund,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-71"><a href="#cite_note-71">[71]</a></sup> Dragør,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-72"><a href="#cite_note-72">[72]</a></sup> Hvidovre,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-73"><a href="#cite_note-73">[73]</a></sup> Rødovre,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-74"><a href="#cite_note-74">[74]</a></sup> Vallensbæk,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-75"><a href="#cite_note-75">[75]</a></sup> and Herlev.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-76"><a href="#cite_note-76">[76]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Estonia">Estonia</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Water_fluoridation_by_country&amp;action=edit&amp;section=24" title="Edit section: Estonia">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>There is no water fluoridation in Estonia.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-OIMextent0_24-5"><a href="#cite_note-OIMextent0-24">[24]</a></sup> About 5% of the population may be exposed to excessive natural fluoride in drinking waters, and there are measures to remove excess fluoride.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-77"><a href="#cite_note-77">[77]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Finland">Finland</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Water_fluoridation_by_country&amp;action=edit&amp;section=25" title="Edit section: Finland">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p><a href="/wiki/Kuopio" title="Kuopio">Kuopio</a> is the only community in Finland with at least 70,000 people that has ever had water fluoridated.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NCFPR_51-2"><a href="#cite_note-NCFPR-51">[51]</a></sup> Kuopio stopped fluoridation in 1992.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Caries_Research_78-0"><a href="#cite_note-Caries_Research-78">[78]</a></sup> In regions with <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Rapakivi" title="Rapakivi">rapakivi</a> bedrock (small, but densely populated regions), 22% of well waters and 55% of drilled well waters exceed the legal limit of 1.5 mg/L; generally, surface and well waters have 0.5-2.0 mg/L fluoride in affected regions.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-79"><a href="#cite_note-79">[79]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="France">France</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Water_fluoridation_by_country&amp;action=edit&amp;section=26" title="Edit section: France">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Fluoridated salt is available in France,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NCFPR_51-3"><a href="#cite_note-NCFPR-51">[51]</a></sup> and 3% of the population uses naturally fluoridated water,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-OIMextent0_24-6"><a href="#cite_note-OIMextent0-24">[24]</a></sup> but the water is not artificially fluoridated.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-OIMextent0_24-7"><a href="#cite_note-OIMextent0-24">[24]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-SLW00_62-1"><a href="#cite_note-SLW00-62">[62]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Germany">Germany</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Water_fluoridation_by_country&amp;action=edit&amp;section=27" title="Edit section: Germany">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Public drinking water supplies are not currently fluoridated in any part of Germany,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-80"><a href="#cite_note-80">[80]</a></sup> however for children and adolescents use of fluoridated salt and toothpaste, as well as fluoride tablets and washes is strongly encouraged by the German Ministry of Health.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-81"><a href="#cite_note-81">[81]</a></sup>
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Kassel" title="Kassel">Kassel</a>-Wahlershausen in <a href="/wiki/West_Germany" title="West Germany">West Germany</a> became the second location in Europe where water fluoridation was practiced in 1952. By 1962, no other part of the FRG was fluoridating,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-EuropeEarlyFluoride_61-1"><a href="#cite_note-EuropeEarlyFluoride-61">[61]</a></sup> and Kassel-Wahlershausen discontinued the practice in 1971.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (April 2021)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup>
</p><p>In the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/German_Democratic_Republic" title="German Democratic Republic">GDR</a> (East Germany) in the late 1980s, about 3.4 million people (20%) were receiving water with added fluoride. <a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Fluoride_tablets&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Fluoride tablets (page does not exist)">Fluoride tablets</a> were also provided.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-bmartin0_82-0"><a href="#cite_note-bmartin0-82">[82]</a></sup> The fluoridated areas of the GDR included the towns of <a href="/wiki/Chemnitz" title="Chemnitz">Karl-Marx-Stadt</a> (now Chemnitz), <a href="/wiki/Plauen" title="Plauen">Plauen</a>, <a href="/wiki/Zittau" title="Zittau">Zittau</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Spremberg" title="Spremberg">Spremberg</a>. Children in those towns were part of large long-running studies of caries prevalence. A fluoride cessation study found that consistent with a previously observed population-wide phenomenon that the rate of cavities continued to drop after the fluoride concentration in water fell from the augmented 1.0 ppm to its natural level below 0.2 ppm.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-83"><a href="#cite_note-83">[83]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-84"><a href="#cite_note-84">[84]</a></sup> Water fluoridation was discontinued after the German reunification although still exists on some US military bases.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Greece">Greece</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Water_fluoridation_by_country&amp;action=edit&amp;section=28" title="Edit section: Greece">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>There is no water fluoridation in Greece.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-85"><a href="#cite_note-85">[85]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Hungary">Hungary</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Water_fluoridation_by_country&amp;action=edit&amp;section=29" title="Edit section: Hungary">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>In the early 1960s the city of <a href="/wiki/Szolnok" title="Szolnok">Szolnok</a> briefly fluoridated its water. The program was discontinued due to technical problems and a public view that fluoridation did not seem reasonable.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="margin-left:0.1em; white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Please_clarify" title="Wikipedia:Please clarify"><span title="The text near this tag may need clarification or removal of jargon. (May 2013)">clarification needed</span></a></i>]</sup> Hungary has not used artificially fluoridated water since then.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (May 2013)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Ireland">Ireland</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Water_fluoridation_by_country&amp;action=edit&amp;section=30" title="Edit section: Ireland">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Ireland is the only European country with a policy of mandatory water fluoridation.  Worldwide, the Irish Republic and <a href="/wiki/Singapore" title="Singapore">Singapore</a> are the only countries which implement mandatory water fluoridation.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-86"><a href="#cite_note-86">[86]</a></sup>
</p><p>The majority of drinking water in the Republic, (but not Northern Ireland), is fluoridated. In 2012, roughly 3.25 million people received artificially-fluoridated water.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-OIMextent0_24-8"><a href="#cite_note-OIMextent0-24">[24]</a></sup> Almost 71% of the population in 2002 resided in fluoridated communities.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-87"><a href="#cite_note-87">[87]</a></sup> All public water supplies are fluoridated and the remainder of the supplies are group water schemes which are privately owned and not fluoridated artificially.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-fluoridefreewater.ie_88-0"><a href="#cite_note-fluoridefreewater.ie-88">[88]</a></sup> The fluoridation agent used is <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Hydrofluorosilicic_acid" title="Hydrofluorosilicic acid">hydrofluorosilicic acid</a> (HFSA; H<sub>2</sub>SiF<sub>6</sub>).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-89"><a href="#cite_note-89">[89]</a></sup> In a 2002 public survey, 45% of respondents expressed some concern about fluoridation.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-90"><a href="#cite_note-90">[90]</a></sup>
</p><p>In 1957, the <a href="/wiki/Department_of_Health_(Ireland)" title="Department of Health (Ireland)">Department of Health</a> established a <i>Fluorine Consultative Council</i> which recommended fluoridation at 1.0 ppm of public water supplies, then accessed by approximately 50% of the population.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOF2002_71_91-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOF2002_71-91">[91]</a></sup> This was felt to be an effective way of preventing tooth decay, in an era before fluoridated toothpaste was commonly used.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-dohcie72_92-0"><a href="#cite_note-dohcie72-92">[92]</a></sup> This led to the <i>Health (Fluoridation of Water Supplies) Act</i> 1960, which mandated compulsory fluoridation by <a href="/wiki/Local_government_in_the_Republic_of_Ireland" title="Local government in the Republic of Ireland">local authorities</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-dohcie72_92-1"><a href="#cite_note-dohcie72-92">[92]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-93"><a href="#cite_note-93">[93]</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/Statutory_instrument" title="Statutory instrument">statutory instruments</a> made in 1962–65 under the 1960 Act were separate for each local authority, setting the level of fluoride in drinking water to 0.8–1.0 ppm.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-94"><a href="#cite_note-94">[94]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-95"><a href="#cite_note-95">[95]</a></sup> The current regulations date from 2007, and set the level to 0.6–0.8 ppm, with a target value of 0.7 ppm.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-96"><a href="#cite_note-96">[96]</a></sup>
</p><p>Implementation of fluoridation was held up by preliminary dental surveying and water testing,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-97"><a href="#cite_note-97">[97]</a></sup> and a court case, <i>Ryan v. Attorney General</i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ryan-ag-1965_98-0"><a href="#cite_note-ryan-ag-1965-98">[98]</a></sup>
</p><p>In 1960, the <a href="/wiki/Fianna_F%C3%A1il" title="Fianna Fáil">Fianna Fáil</a> minister for health, <a href="/wiki/Se%C3%A1n_MacEntee" title="Seán MacEntee">Seán MacEntee</a>, brought forward the <i>Health (Fluoridation of Water Supplies) Act</i> and a <a href="/wiki/Dublin" title="Dublin">Dublin</a> housewife Gladys Ryan challenged the Act as an “invasion of family rights”.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Irish_Times_99-0"><a href="#cite_note-Irish_Times-99">[99]</a></sup> Ryan lost the case, which lasted 65 days, at the <a href="/wiki/High_Court_(Ireland)" title="High Court (Ireland)">High Court (Ireland)</a>, and appealed to the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Supreme_Court_(Ireland)" title="Supreme Court (Ireland)">Supreme Court</a>. Ryan was represented in court by <a href="/wiki/Se%C3%A1n_MacBride" title="Seán MacBride">Seán MacBride</a> who  argued that fluoridation was an infringement of <a href="/wiki/Human_rights" title="Human rights">human rights</a> since people had no option but to drink it. Ryan's lawyers, including <a href="/wiki/Richie_Ryan_(politician)" title="Richie Ryan (politician)">Richie Ryan (politician)</a> worked on a pro bono basis and expenses were paid by fundraising.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Irish_Times_99-1"><a href="#cite_note-Irish_Times-99">[99]</a></sup> In 1965, the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Supreme_Court_(Ireland)" title="Supreme Court (Ireland)">Supreme Court</a> rejected Gladys Ryan's appeal that the Act violated the <a href="/wiki/Constitution_of_Ireland" title="Constitution of Ireland">Constitution of Ireland</a>'s guarantee of the right to bodily integrity.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ryan-ag-1965_98-1"><a href="#cite_note-ryan-ag-1965-98">[98]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-100"><a href="#cite_note-100">[100]</a></sup>
</p><p>By 1965, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Greater_Dublin" title="Greater Dublin">Greater Dublin</a>'s water was fluoridated; by 1973, other urban centers were too.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-101"><a href="#cite_note-101">[101]</a></sup> Studies from the late 1970s to mid 1990s showed a decrease in (and lower incidence of) dental decay in school children living in areas where water was fluoridated than in areas where water was not fluoridated.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-102"><a href="#cite_note-102">[102]</a></sup> The government of the Republic of Ireland has yet to carry out a <a href="/wiki/Public_health" title="Public health">public health</a> survey on the effects of fluoridation, even though this is required to under the 1960 <i>Health (Fluoridation of Water Supplies) Act</i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-103"><a href="#cite_note-103">[103]</a></sup>
</p><p>A <a href="/wiki/Private_member%27s_bill" title="Private member's bill">private member's bill</a> to end fluoridation was defeated in the Dáil on 12 November 2013.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-104"><a href="#cite_note-104">[104]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-dail20131112_105-0"><a href="#cite_note-dail20131112-105">[105]</a></sup> It was supported by <a href="/wiki/Sinn_F%C3%A9in" title="Sinn Féin">Sinn Féin</a> and some of the <a href="/wiki/Technical_group" title="Technical group">technical group</a> and opposed by the <a href="/wiki/Government_of_the_31st_D%C3%A1il" title="Government of the 31st Dáil">Fine Gael-Labour government</a> and <a href="/wiki/Fianna_F%C3%A1il" title="Fianna Fáil">Fianna Fáil</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-dail20131112_105-1"><a href="#cite_note-dail20131112-105">[105]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-106"><a href="#cite_note-106">[106]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-107"><a href="#cite_note-107">[107]</a></sup>
</p><p>There is much local government opposition to compulsory fluoridation, legally mandated nationwide by <a href="/wiki/D%C3%A1il_%C3%89ireann" title="Dáil Éireann">Dáil Éireann</a>. Early in 2014, <a href="/wiki/Cork_County_Council" title="Cork County Council">Cork County Council</a> and <a href="/wiki/Laois_County_Council" title="Laois County Council">Laois County Council</a> passed motions for the cessation of water fluoridation. In Autumn 2014, <a href="/wiki/Cork_City_Council" title="Cork City Council">Cork City Council</a>, <a href="/wiki/Dublin_City_Council" title="Dublin City Council">Dublin City Council</a> and <a href="/wiki/Kerry_County_Council" title="Kerry County Council">Kerry County Council</a> passed similar motions.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-108"><a href="#cite_note-108">[108]</a></sup> However, because of the 1960 law forcing artificial fluoridation of the public water, city councils and corporations can only vote to stop fluoridation but have no power to stop it, unless the law is repealed.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-fluoridefreewater.ie_88-1"><a href="#cite_note-fluoridefreewater.ie-88">[88]</a></sup>
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Fine_Gael" title="Fine Gael">Fine Gael</a> was opposed to compulsory water fluoridation but they now support the policy.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-109"><a href="#cite_note-109">[109]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Fianna_F%C3%A1il" title="Fianna Fáil">Fianna Fáil</a> is in favour of compulsory water fluoridation and in 2004 <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Micheal_Martin" title="Micheal Martin">Micheal Martin</a> set up the pro-fluoride <i>Irish Expert Body on Fluorides and Health</i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-110"><a href="#cite_note-110">[110]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Italy">Italy</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Water_fluoridation_by_country&amp;action=edit&amp;section=31" title="Edit section: Italy">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>There is no water or food fluoridation in Italy. Except for isolated locations near volcanos or polluters, fluoride in water is low across the country.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-111"><a href="#cite_note-111">[111]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Latvia">Latvia</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Water_fluoridation_by_country&amp;action=edit&amp;section=32" title="Edit section: Latvia">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>There is no water fluoridation in Latvia.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-OIMextent0_24-9"><a href="#cite_note-OIMextent0-24">[24]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Riga" title="Riga">Riga</a>'s upper limit on natural fluoride is 1.5 mg/L.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Fluoride_112-0"><a href="#cite_note-Fluoride-112">[112]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Netherlands">Netherlands</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Water_fluoridation_by_country&amp;action=edit&amp;section=33" title="Edit section: Netherlands">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Water was fluoridated in large parts of the Netherlands from 1960 to 1973, when the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Hoge_Raad_der_Nederlanden" title="Hoge Raad der Nederlanden">High Council of The Netherlands</a> declared fluoridation of drinking water unauthorized.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-113"><a href="#cite_note-113">[113]</a></sup> Dutch authorities had no legal basis for adding chemicals to drinking water if they would not contribute to a sound water supply.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Bestuursrecht_1,_2de_druk_114-0"><a href="#cite_note-Bestuursrecht_1,_2de_druk-114">[114]</a></sup> Drinking water has not been fluoridated in any part of the Netherlands since 1973.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Norway">Norway</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Water_fluoridation_by_country&amp;action=edit&amp;section=34" title="Edit section: Norway">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>In 2000, representatives of the <a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Norwegian_National_Institute_for_Public_Health&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Norwegian National Institute for Public Health (page does not exist)">Norwegian National Institute for Public Health</a> reported that no cities in Norway were practicing water fluoridation. There had been intense discussion of the issue around 1980, but no ongoing political discussion in 2000.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NorwayNIPH00_115-0"><a href="#cite_note-NorwayNIPH00-115">[115]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Serbia">Serbia</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Water_fluoridation_by_country&amp;action=edit&amp;section=35" title="Edit section: Serbia">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>About 300,000 people in Serbia (3%) were receiving fluoridated water before 2003.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-OIMextent0_24-10"><a href="#cite_note-OIMextent0-24">[24]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Spain">Spain</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Water_fluoridation_by_country&amp;action=edit&amp;section=36" title="Edit section: Spain">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Around 10% of the population<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-PMID_16215546_116-0"><a href="#cite_note-PMID_16215546-116">[116]</a></sup> (4,250,000 people)<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-OIMextent0_24-11"><a href="#cite_note-OIMextent0-24">[24]</a></sup> receive fluoridated water.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Sweden">Sweden</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Water_fluoridation_by_country&amp;action=edit&amp;section=37" title="Edit section: Sweden">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>In 1952, <a href="/wiki/Norrk%C3%B6ping" title="Norrköping">Norrköping</a> in Sweden became one of the first cities in Europe to fluoridate its water supply.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Larsson1981_117-0"><a href="#cite_note-Larsson1981-117">[117]</a></sup> It was declared illegal by the <a href="/wiki/Supreme_Administrative_Court_of_Sweden" title="Supreme Administrative Court of Sweden">Supreme Administrative Court of Sweden</a> in 1961, re-legalized in 1962<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-118"><a href="#cite_note-118">[118]</a></sup> and finally prohibited by the parliament in 1971,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-119"><a href="#cite_note-119">[119]</a></sup> after considerable debate. The parliament majority said that there were other and better ways of reducing tooth decay than water fluoridation. Four cities received permission to fluoridate tap water when it was legal.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Larsson1981_117-1"><a href="#cite_note-Larsson1981-117">[117]</a></sup><sup class="reference nowrap"><span title="Page / location: 56–57">: 56–57 </span></sup> An official commission was formed, which published its final report in 1981. They recommended other ways of reducing tooth decay (improving food and <a href="/wiki/Oral_hygiene" title="Oral hygiene">oral hygiene</a> habits) instead of fluoridating tap water. They also found that many people found fluoridation to infringe upon personal liberty/<a href="/wiki/Freedom_of_choice" title="Freedom of choice">freedom of choice</a> by forcing them to be medicated, and that the long-term effects of fluoridation were insufficiently acknowledged. They also lacked a proper study on the effects of fluoridation on formula-fed infants.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Larsson1981_117-2"><a href="#cite_note-Larsson1981-117">[117]</a></sup><sup class="reference nowrap"><span title="Page / location: 29">: 29 </span></sup> In the year 2004 the allowed amount of fluoride in the water was decreased to 1,5 mg/L.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-120"><a href="#cite_note-120">[120]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Switzerland">Switzerland</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Water_fluoridation_by_country&amp;action=edit&amp;section=38" title="Edit section: Switzerland">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>In Switzerland, since 1962, two fluoridation programs had operated in tandem: water fluoridation in the City of <a href="/wiki/Basel" title="Basel">Basel</a>, and salt fluoridation in the rest of Switzerland (around 83% of domestic salt sold had fluoride added). However it became increasingly difficult to keep the two programs separate. As a result, some of the population of Basel were assumed to use both fluoridated salt and fluoridated water. In order to correct the situation, in April 2003 the <a href="/wiki/Grand_Council_of_Basel-Stadt" title="Grand Council of Basel-Stadt">Grand Council of Basel-Stadt</a> resolved to cease water fluoridation and expand salt fluoridation to Basel.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-121"><a href="#cite_note-121">[121]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-newsch00_122-0"><a href="#cite_note-newsch00-122">[122]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="United_Kingdom">United Kingdom</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Water_fluoridation_by_country&amp;action=edit&amp;section=39" title="Edit section: United Kingdom">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Around 14% of the population of the United Kingdom receives fluoridated water.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-PMID_16215546_116-1"><a href="#cite_note-PMID_16215546-116">[116]</a></sup> About half a million people receive water that is naturally fluoridated with <a href="/wiki/Calcium_fluoride" title="Calcium fluoride">calcium fluoride</a>, and about 6 million total receive fluoridated water.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Gibson-Moore_123-0"><a href="#cite_note-Gibson-Moore-123">[123]</a></sup> The Water Act 2003 required water suppliers to comply with requests from local health authorities to fluoridate their water.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Gibson-Moore_123-1"><a href="#cite_note-Gibson-Moore-123">[123]</a></sup>
</p><p>The following UK water utility companies fluoridate their supply:
</p>
<ul><li><a href="/wiki/Anglian_Water" title="Anglian Water">Anglian Water</a> Services Ltd</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Northumbrian_Water" title="Northumbrian Water">Northumbrian Water</a> Ltd</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/South_Staffordshire_Water" title="South Staffordshire Water">South Staffordshire Water</a> plc</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Severn_Trent" title="Severn Trent">Severn Trent</a> plc</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/United_Utilities" title="United Utilities">United Utilities</a> Water plc</li></ul>
<p>Earlier plans were undertaken in the Health Authority areas of <a href="/wiki/Bedfordshire" title="Bedfordshire">Bedfordshire</a>, <a href="/wiki/Hertfordshire" title="Hertfordshire">Hertfordshire</a>, <a href="/wiki/Birmingham" title="Birmingham">Birmingham</a>, <a href="/wiki/Black_Country" title="Black Country">Black Country</a>, <a href="/wiki/Cheshire" title="Cheshire">Cheshire</a>, <a href="/wiki/Merseyside" title="Merseyside">Merseyside</a>, <a href="/wiki/County_Durham" title="County Durham">County Durham</a>, <a href="/wiki/Tees_Valley" title="Tees Valley">Tees Valley</a>, <a href="/wiki/Cumbria" title="Cumbria">Cumbria</a>, <a href="/wiki/Lancashire" title="Lancashire">Lancashire</a>, <a href="/wiki/North_Yorkshire" title="North Yorkshire">North</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/East_Yorkshire" title="East Yorkshire">East Yorkshire</a>, Northern <a href="/wiki/Lincolnshire" title="Lincolnshire">Lincolnshire</a>, <a href="/wiki/Northumberland" title="Northumberland">Northumberland</a>, <a href="/wiki/Tyne_and_Wear" title="Tyne and Wear">Tyne and Wear</a>, <a href="/wiki/Shropshire" title="Shropshire">Shropshire</a>, <a href="/wiki/Staffordshire" title="Staffordshire">Staffordshire</a>, Trent and <a href="/wiki/West_Midlands_(region)" title="West Midlands (region)">West Midlands</a> South whereby fluoridation was introduced progressively in the years between 1964 and 1988.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-124"><a href="#cite_note-124">[124]</a></sup>
</p><p>The South Central Strategic Health Authority carried out the first public consultation under the Water Act 2003, and in 2009 its board voted to fluoridate water supplies in the <a href="/wiki/Southampton" title="Southampton">Southampton</a> area to address the high incidence of tooth decay in children there.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Gibson-Moore_123-2"><a href="#cite_note-Gibson-Moore-123">[123]</a></sup> Surveys had found that the majority of surveyed Southampton residents opposed the plan, but the Southampton City Primary Care Trust decided that "public vote could not be the deciding factor and that medical evidence shows fluoridation will reduce tooth decay – and failed to back up claims of serious negative side effects".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-125"><a href="#cite_note-125">[125]</a></sup> Fluoridation plans in the northwest of England were delayed after concerns over increased projected costs and health risks were raised.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-126"><a href="#cite_note-126">[126]</a></sup> In October 2014, <a href="/wiki/Public_Health_England" title="Public Health England">Public Health England</a> abandoned plans for water fluoridation for 195,000 people in Southampton and neighbouring parts of south-west Hampshire due to opposition from both Hampshire County Council and Southampton City Council.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-127"><a href="#cite_note-127">[127]</a></sup>
</p><p>It was reported in 2007 that the UK Milk Fluoridation Programme, centered in the northwest of England, involved more than 16,000 children.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-OHSNI00_128-0"><a href="#cite_note-OHSNI00-128">[128]</a></sup>
</p><p>The water supply in Northern Ireland has never been artificially fluoridated except in two small localities where fluoride was added to the water for about 30 years. By 1999, fluoridation ceased in those two areas, as well.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (May 2012)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup>
</p><p>In 2004, following a public consultation, Scotland's parliament rejected proposals to fluoridate public drinking water.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-BBCscot00_63-1"><a href="#cite_note-BBCscot00-63">[63]</a></sup>
</p><p>There are currently no community fluoridation schemes in Wales. The Welsh Government stated in November 2014 that it had no plans to fluoridate the water supply, but said that it was something the Welsh Government will continue to review.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-129"><a href="#cite_note-129">[129]</a></sup>
</p><p>In September 2021, the UK's chief medical officers concluded that fluoridation of water supplies would cut tooth decay.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-130"><a href="#cite_note-130">[130]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="North_America">North America</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Water_fluoridation_by_country&amp;action=edit&amp;section=40" title="Edit section: North America">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Canada">Canada</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Water_fluoridation_by_country&amp;action=edit&amp;section=41" title="Edit section: Canada">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>The decision to fluoridate lies with local governments, with guidelines set by provincial, territorial, and federal governments. Brantford, Ontario, became the first city in Canada to fluoridate its water supplies in 1945.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Rabb-Waytowich_131-0"><a href="#cite_note-Rabb-Waytowich-131">[131]</a></sup> In 1955, Toronto approved water fluoridation, but delayed implementation of the program until 1963 due to a campaign against fluoridation by broadcaster <a href="/wiki/Gordon_Sinclair" title="Gordon Sinclair">Gordon Sinclair</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-132"><a href="#cite_note-132">[132]</a></sup> The city continues to fluoridate its water today.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-133"><a href="#cite_note-133">[133]</a></sup> In 2008 the recommended fluoride levels in Canada were reduced from 0.8 to 1.0 mg/L to 0.7 mg/L to minimize the risk of dental fluorosis. Ontario, Alberta, and Manitoba have the highest rates of fluoridation, about 70–75%. The lowest rates are in Quebec (about 6%), British Columbia (about 4% - Vancouver does not add Fluoride), and Newfoundland and Labrador (1.5%), with Nunavut and the Yukon having no fluoridation at all.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Rabb-Waytowich_131-1"><a href="#cite_note-Rabb-Waytowich-131">[131]</a></sup> Overall, about 45% of the <a href="/wiki/Canadians" title="Canadians">Canadian</a> population had access to fluoridated water supplies in 2007.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Rabb-Waytowich_131-2"><a href="#cite_note-Rabb-Waytowich-131">[131]</a></sup> A 2008 telephone survey found that about half of Canadian adults knew about fluoridation, and of these, 62% supported the idea.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-134"><a href="#cite_note-134">[134]</a></sup>
</p><p>In 2010, the <a href="/wiki/Regional_Municipality_of_Waterloo" title="Regional Municipality of Waterloo">Region of Waterloo</a> held a non-binding referendum for residents to decide whether water fluoridation should continue.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-135"><a href="#cite_note-135">[135]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ctvnws_136-0"><a href="#cite_note-ctvnws-136">[136]</a></sup> The result of the vote was 50.3% voting against fluoridation.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ctvnws_136-1"><a href="#cite_note-ctvnws-136">[136]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-137"><a href="#cite_note-137">[137]</a></sup> The regional council honored the vote, and over forty years of fluoridation in Waterloo Region ended in November.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-138"><a href="#cite_note-138">[138]</a></sup>
</p><p>In 2011, <a href="/wiki/Calgary" title="Calgary">Calgary</a> city council voted 10–3 to stop adding fluoride to the city's drinking water, having started water fluoridation in 1991.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-CBCcalgary_139-0"><a href="#cite_note-CBCcalgary-139">[139]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-CBCcalgary1_140-0"><a href="#cite_note-CBCcalgary1-140">[140]</a></sup> A research project has been planned to study the effects of Calgary's cessation, using <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Edmonton,_Alberta" title="Edmonton, Alberta">Edmonton</a> as a control.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-141"><a href="#cite_note-141">[141]</a></sup>
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Lakeshore,_Ontario" title="Lakeshore, Ontario">Lakeshore</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Amherstburg,_Ontario" title="Amherstburg, Ontario">Amherstberg</a> have voted to end water fluoridation.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-windsor0_142-0"><a href="#cite_note-windsor0-142">[142]</a></sup>
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Hamilton,_Ontario" title="Hamilton, Ontario">Hamilton</a>, <a href="/wiki/London,_Ontario" title="London, Ontario">London</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Toronto" title="Toronto">Toronto</a> have recently chosen to continue fluoridation. Toronto treats its water to 0.6 mg/L.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-143"><a href="#cite_note-143">[143]</a></sup>
</p><p>On 28 January 2013, <a href="/wiki/Windsor,_Ontario" title="Windsor, Ontario">Windsor</a> city council voted 8–3 to cease fluoridation of Windsor's drinking water for five years, honoring a February 2012 recommendation by the Windsor Utilities Commission. <a href="/wiki/Tecumseh,_Ontario" title="Tecumseh, Ontario">Tecumseh</a> gets its water from Windsor, and Tecmuseh's council had voted on 13 March 2012 to ask Windsor to stop fluoridating. Money formerly spent on fluoridation was reallocated to oral health and nutrition education programs. Windsor's water had been fluoridated for over fifty years.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-windsor0_142-1"><a href="#cite_note-windsor0-142">[142]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-windsor1_144-0"><a href="#cite_note-windsor1-144">[144]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-145"><a href="#cite_note-145">[145]</a></sup>
</p><p>On 14 December 2018, <a href="/wiki/Windsor,_Ontario" title="Windsor, Ontario">Windsor</a> city council voted 8–3 to reintroduce fluoridation of Windsor's drinking water. According to the Oral Health 2018 report released by the health unit, the percentage of children with tooth decay or requiring urgent care has increased by 51 per cent in 2016–17 compared to 2011–12.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-windsor2_146-0"><a href="#cite_note-windsor2-146">[146]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Mexico">Mexico</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Water_fluoridation_by_country&amp;action=edit&amp;section=42" title="Edit section: Mexico">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Mexico has no water fluoridation program; instead it has a table salt fluoridation program. But the potable water in Mexico City has higher levels of fluor than recommended by WHO.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-147"><a href="#cite_note-147">[147]</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:US-fluoridation-1992-2006.jpeg"><img class="thumbimage" data-file-height="751" data-file-width="510" decoding="async" height="324" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7c/US-fluoridation-1992-2006.jpeg/220px-US-fluoridation-1992-2006.jpeg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7c/US-fluoridation-1992-2006.jpeg/330px-US-fluoridation-1992-2006.jpeg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7c/US-fluoridation-1992-2006.jpeg/440px-US-fluoridation-1992-2006.jpeg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:US-fluoridation-1992-2006.jpeg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>U.S. residents served with community water fluoridation, 1992 and 2006. The percentages are the proportions of the resident population served by public water supplies who are receiving fluoridated water.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-148"><a href="#cite_note-148">[148]</a></sup></div></div></div>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="United_States">United States</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Water_fluoridation_by_country&amp;action=edit&amp;section=43" title="Edit section: United States">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r1033289096">.mw-parser-output .hatnote{font-style:italic}.mw-parser-output div.hatnote{padding-left:1.6em;margin-bottom:0.5em}.mw-parser-output .hatnote i{font-style:normal}.mw-parser-output .hatnote+link+.hatnote{margin-top:-0.5em}</style><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Water_fluoridation_in_the_United_States" title="Water fluoridation in the United States">Water fluoridation in the United States</a></div>
<p>As of May 2000, 42 of the 50 largest U.S. cities had water fluoridation.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-CDC1_149-0"><a href="#cite_note-CDC1-149">[149]</a></sup> In 2010, 66% of all U.S. residents and 74% of U.S. residents with access to community water systems receive fluoridated water.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-CDC2_150-0"><a href="#cite_note-CDC2-150">[150]</a></sup> In 2010, a U.S. <a href="/wiki/Centers_for_Disease_Control_and_Prevention" title="Centers for Disease Control and Prevention">Centers for Disease Control and Prevention</a> study determined that "40.7% of adolescents aged 12–15 had dental fluorosis [in 1999–2004]".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-cdc.gov_151-0"><a href="#cite_note-cdc.gov-151">[151]</a></sup> In response, in 2011 the U.S. <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Department_of_Health_and_Human_Services" title="Department of Health and Human Services">Department of Health and Human Services</a> and the U.S. <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Environmental_Protection_Agency" title="Environmental Protection Agency">Environmental Protection Agency</a> (EPA) proposed to reduce the recommended level of fluoride in drinking water to the lowest end of the current range, 0.7 milligrams per liter of water (mg/L), from the previous recommended maximum of 0.7 to 1.2 mg/L in recognition of the increase in sources of fluoride such as fluoridated toothpastes and mouthwashes.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ReferenceA_152-0"><a href="#cite_note-ReferenceA-152">[152]</a></sup><sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Verifiability" title="Wikipedia:Verifiability"><span title="The material near this tag failed verification of its source citation(s). (April 2020)">failed verification</span></a></i>]</sup> <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-153"><a href="#cite_note-153">[153]</a></sup> This could effectively terminate municipal water fluoridation in areas where fluoride levels from mineral deposits and industrial pollution exceed the new recommendation.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-154"><a href="#cite_note-154">[154]</a></sup> As of 2021 the federal <a href="/wiki/Maximum_contaminant_level" title="Maximum contaminant level">maximum contaminant level</a> for fluoride in public water systems remains at 4.0 mg/L, which had been promulgated by EPA in 1986.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-EPA_DW_contaminants_155-0"><a href="#cite_note-EPA_DW_contaminants-155">[155]</a></sup> Several states have set more stringent standards, including <a href="/wiki/New_York_(state)" title="New York (state)">New York</a>, where the fluoride MCL is 2.2 mg/L.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-156"><a href="#cite_note-156">[156]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Oceania">Oceania</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Water_fluoridation_by_country&amp;action=edit&amp;section=44" title="Edit section: Oceania">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Australia">Australia</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Water_fluoridation_by_country&amp;action=edit&amp;section=45" title="Edit section: Australia">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
